From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Number divisible only by 1 or itself

"Prime" redirects here. For other uses, see [Prime (disambiguation)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_(disambiguation) "Prime (disambiguation)").

[![Image 6: Groups of two to twelve dots, showing that the composite numbers of dots (4, 6, 8, 9, 10, and 12) can be arranged into rectangles but prime numbers cannot](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Primes-vs-composites.svg/250px-Primes-vs-composites.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Primes-vs-composites.svg)

[Composite numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Composite_number "Composite number") can be arranged into [rectangles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rectangle "Rectangle") but prime numbers cannot.

A **prime number** (or a **prime**) is a [natural number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_number "Natural number") greater than 1 that is not a [product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Product_(mathematics) "Product (mathematics)") of two smaller natural numbers. A natural number greater than 1 that is not prime is called a [composite number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Composite_number "Composite number"). For example, 5 is prime because the only ways of writing it as a product, 1 × 5 or 5 × 1, involve 5 itself. However, 4 is composite because it is a product (2×2) in which both numbers are smaller than 4. Primes are central in [number theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_theory "Number theory") because of the [fundamental theorem of arithmetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_arithmetic "Fundamental theorem of arithmetic"): every natural number greater than 1 is either a prime itself or can be [factorized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Factorization "Factorization") as a product of primes that is unique [up to](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Up_to "Up to") their order.

The property of being prime is called **primality**. A simple but slow [method of checking the primality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primality_test "Primality test") of a given number ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 7: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠, called [trial division](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trial_division "Trial division"), tests whether ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 8: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ is a multiple of any integer between 2 and ⁠n{\displaystyle {\sqrt {n}}}![Image 9: {\displaystyle {\sqrt {n}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2a2994734eae382ce30100fb17b9447fd8e99f81)⁠. Faster algorithms include the [Miller–Rabin primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Rabin_primality_test "Miller–Rabin primality test"), which is fast but has a small chance of error, and the [AKS primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AKS_primality_test "AKS primality test"), which always produces the correct answer in [polynomial time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polynomial_time "Polynomial time") but is too slow to be practical. Particularly fast methods are available for numbers of special forms, such as [Mersenne numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mersenne_number "Mersenne number"). As of October 2024[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Prime_number&action=edit) the [largest known prime number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Largest_known_prime_number "Largest known prime number") is a Mersenne prime with 41,024,320 [decimal digits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numerical_digit "Numerical digit").[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-GIMPS-2024-1)[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-2)

There are [infinitely many](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_set "Infinite set") primes, as [demonstrated by Euclid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid%27s_theorem "Euclid's theorem") around 300 BC. No known simple formula separates prime numbers from composite numbers. However, the distribution of primes within the natural numbers in the large can be statistically modelled. The first result in that direction is the [prime number theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number_theorem "Prime number theorem"), proven at the end of the 19th century, which says roughly that the [probability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probability "Probability") of a randomly chosen large number being prime is inversely [proportional](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics) "Proportionality (mathematics)") to its number of digits, that is, to its [logarithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logarithm "Logarithm").

Several historical questions regarding prime numbers are still unsolved. These include [Goldbach's conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goldbach%27s_conjecture "Goldbach's conjecture"), that every even integer greater than 2 can be expressed as the sum of two primes, and the [twin prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twin_prime "Twin prime") conjecture, that there are infinitely many pairs of primes that differ by two. Such questions spurred the development of various branches of number theory, focusing on [analytic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Analytic_number_theory "Analytic number theory") or [algebraic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_number_theory "Algebraic number theory") aspects of numbers. Primes are used in several routines in [information technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_technology "Information technology"), such as [public-key cryptography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public-key_cryptography "Public-key cryptography"), which relies on the difficulty of [factoring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer_factorization "Integer factorization") large numbers into their prime factors. In [abstract algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstract_algebra "Abstract algebra"), objects that behave in a generalized way like prime numbers include [prime elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_element "Prime element") and [prime ideals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_ideal "Prime ideal").

Definition and examples
-----------------------

Main article: [List of prime numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_prime_numbers "List of prime numbers")

A [natural number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_number "Natural number") (1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, etc.) is called a _prime number_ (or a _prime_) if it is greater than 1 and cannot be written as the product of two smaller natural numbers. The numbers greater than 1 that are not prime are called [composite numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Composite_number "Composite number").[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-3) In other words, ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 10: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ is prime if ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 11: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ items cannot be divided up into smaller equal-size groups of more than one item,[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-4) or if it is not possible to arrange ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 12: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ dots into a rectangular grid that is more than one dot wide and more than one dot high.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-5) For example, among the numbers 1 through 6, the numbers 2, 3, and 5 are the prime numbers,[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-6) as there are no other numbers that divide them evenly (without a remainder). 1 is not prime, as it is specifically excluded in the definition. 4 = 2 × 2 and 6 = 2 × 3 are both composite.

[![Image 13: refer to caption](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Prime_number_Cuisenaire_rods_7.png/330px-Prime_number_Cuisenaire_rods_7.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Prime_number_Cuisenaire_rods_7.png)

Demonstration, with [Cuisenaire rods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuisenaire_rods "Cuisenaire rods"), that 7 is prime, because none of 2, 3, 4, 5, or 6 divide it evenly

The [divisors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divisor "Divisor") of a natural number ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 14: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ are the natural numbers that divide ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 15: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ evenly. Every natural number has both 1 and itself as a divisor. If it has any other divisor, it cannot be prime. This leads to an equivalent definition of prime numbers: they are the numbers with exactly two positive [divisors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divisor "Divisor"). Those two are 1 and the number itself. As 1 has only one divisor, itself, it is not prime by this definition.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-7) Yet another way to express the same thing is that a number ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 16: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ is prime if it is greater than one and if none of the numbers 2,3,…,n−1{\displaystyle 2,3,\dots ,n-1}![Image 17: {\displaystyle 2,3,\dots ,n-1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bc661ca19f3a62fb3fcf54e9e2ba22bfae50edda) divides ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 18: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ evenly.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-8)

The first 25 prime numbers (all the prime numbers less than 100) are:[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-ziegler-9)

[2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2 "2"), [3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/3 "3"), [5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/5 "5"), [7](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/7 "7"), [11](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/11_(number) "11 (number)"), [13](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/13_(number) "13 (number)"), [17](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/17_(number) "17 (number)"), [19](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/19_(number) "19 (number)"), [23](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/23_(number) "23 (number)"), [29](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/29_(number) "29 (number)"), [31](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/31_(number) "31 (number)"), [37](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/37_(number) "37 (number)"), [41](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/41_(number) "41 (number)"), [43](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/43_(number) "43 (number)"), [47](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/47_(number) "47 (number)"), [53](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/53_(number) "53 (number)"), [59](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/59_(number) "59 (number)"), [61](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/61_(number) "61 (number)"), [67](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/67_(number) "67 (number)"), [71](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/71_(number) "71 (number)"), [73](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/73_(number) "73 (number)"), [79](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/79_(number) "79 (number)"), [83](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/83_(number) "83 (number)"), [89](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/89_(number) "89 (number)"), [97](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/97_(number) "97 (number)") (sequence [A000040](https://oeis.org/A000040 "oeis:A000040") in the [OEIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On-Line_Encyclopedia_of_Integer_Sequences "On-Line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences")).
No [even number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Even_number "Even number")⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 19: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ greater than 2 is prime because any such number can be expressed as the product ⁠2×n/2{\displaystyle 2\times n/2}![Image 20: {\displaystyle 2\times n/2}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/06b3d623bd1e97df55c447f8b187c7a62418064c)⁠. Therefore, every prime number other than 2 is an [odd number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Odd_number "Odd number"), and is called an _odd prime_.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-10) Similarly, when written in the usual [decimal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decimal "Decimal") system, all prime numbers larger than 5 end in 1, 3, 7, or 9. The numbers that end with other digits are all composite: decimal numbers that end in 0, 2, 4, 6, or 8 are even, and decimal numbers that end in 0 or 5 are divisible by 5.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-11)

The [set](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics) "Set (mathematics)") of all primes is sometimes denoted by P{\displaystyle \mathbf {P} }![Image 21: {\displaystyle \mathbf {P} }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c0c250ef2a112c86b93c637dfa288c6d7f34ac3f) (a [boldface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boldface "Boldface") capital P)[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-12) or by P{\displaystyle \mathbb {P} }![Image 22: {\displaystyle \mathbb {P} }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1053af9e662ceaf56c4455f90e0f67273422eded) (a [blackboard bold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blackboard_bold "Blackboard bold") capital P).[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-13)

History
-------

[![Image 23: The Rhind Mathematical Papyrus](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Rhind_Mathematical_Papyrus.jpg/250px-Rhind_Mathematical_Papyrus.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Rhind_Mathematical_Papyrus.jpg)

The [Rhind Mathematical Papyrus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhind_Mathematical_Papyrus "Rhind Mathematical Papyrus")

The [Rhind Mathematical Papyrus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhind_Mathematical_Papyrus "Rhind Mathematical Papyrus"), from around 1550 BC, has [Egyptian fraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egyptian_fraction "Egyptian fraction") expansions of different forms for prime and composite numbers.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-14) However, the earliest surviving records of the study of prime numbers come from the [ancient Greek mathematicians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mathematics "Greek mathematics"), who called them _prōtos arithmòs_ (πρῶτος ἀριθμὸς). [Euclid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid "Euclid")'s _[Elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid%27s\_Elements "Euclid's Elements")_ (c. 300 BC) proves the [infinitude of primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinitude_of_primes "Infinitude of primes") and the [fundamental theorem of arithmetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_arithmetic "Fundamental theorem of arithmetic"), and shows how to construct a [perfect number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perfect_number "Perfect number") from a [Mersenne prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mersenne_prime "Mersenne prime").[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-stillwell-2010-p40-15) Another Greek invention, the [Sieve of Eratosthenes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sieve_of_Eratosthenes "Sieve of Eratosthenes"), is still used to construct lists of primes.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-pomerance-sciam-16)[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-mollin-17)

Around 1000 AD, the [Islamic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam "Mathematics in medieval Islam") mathematician [Ibn al-Haytham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham "Ibn al-Haytham") (Alhazen) found [Wilson's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilson%27s_theorem "Wilson's theorem"), characterizing the prime numbers as the numbers ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 24: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ that evenly divide ⁠(n−1)!+1{\displaystyle (n-1)!+1}![Image 25: {\displaystyle (n-1)!+1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5241d2022a4a3e94d24081a5cfb9b7b5fbf98b16)⁠. He also conjectured that all even perfect numbers come from Euclid's construction using Mersenne primes, but was unable to prove it.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-18) Another Islamic mathematician, [Ibn al-Banna' al-Marrakushi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_al-Banna%27_al-Marrakushi "Ibn al-Banna' al-Marrakushi"), observed that the sieve of Eratosthenes can be sped up by considering only the prime divisors up to the square root of the upper limit.[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-mollin-17)[Fibonacci](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci "Fibonacci") took the innovations from Islamic mathematics to Europe. His book _[Liber Abaci](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liber\_Abaci "Liber Abaci")_ (1202) was the first to describe [trial division](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trial_division "Trial division") for testing primality, again using divisors only up to the square root.[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-mollin-17)

In 1640 [Pierre de Fermat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat "Pierre de Fermat") stated (without proof) [Fermat's little theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat%27s_little_theorem "Fermat's little theorem") (later proved by [Leibniz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz "Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz") and [Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler "Leonhard Euler")).[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-19) Fermat also investigated the primality of the [Fermat numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat_number "Fermat number")⁠2 2 n+1{\displaystyle 2^{2^{n}}+1}![Image 26: {\displaystyle 2^{2^{n}}+1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b27f57a4191be088259902a790ef2fb093ffb812)⁠,[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-20) and [Marin Mersenne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marin_Mersenne "Marin Mersenne") studied the [Mersenne primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mersenne_prime "Mersenne prime"), prime numbers of the form 2 p−1{\displaystyle 2^{p}-1}![Image 27: {\displaystyle 2^{p}-1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d5c5977dbf385ba719fbb90f67b0a3d91e1da6d9) with ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 28: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ itself a prime.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-21)[Christian Goldbach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Goldbach "Christian Goldbach") formulated [Goldbach's conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goldbach%27s_conjecture "Goldbach's conjecture"), that every even number is the sum of two primes, in a 1742 letter to Euler.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-22) Euler proved Alhazen's conjecture (now the [Euclid–Euler theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid%E2%80%93Euler_theorem "Euclid–Euler theorem")) that all even perfect numbers can be constructed from Mersenne primes.[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-stillwell-2010-p40-15) He introduced methods from [mathematical analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis "Mathematical analysis") to this area in his proofs of the infinitude of the primes and the [divergence of the sum of the reciprocals of the primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divergence_of_the_sum_of_the_reciprocals_of_the_primes "Divergence of the sum of the reciprocals of the primes")⁠1 2+1 3+1 5+1 7+1 11+⋯{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}+{\tfrac {1}{3}}+{\tfrac {1}{5}}+{\tfrac {1}{7}}+{\tfrac {1}{11}}+\cdots }![Image 29: {\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}+{\tfrac {1}{3}}+{\tfrac {1}{5}}+{\tfrac {1}{7}}+{\tfrac {1}{11}}+\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e57e94d60efb5ad04a9ddebe807c98fff5d6d2a4)⁠.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-23) At the start of the 19th century, Legendre and Gauss conjectured that as ⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 30: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠ tends to infinity, the number of primes up to ⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 31: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠ is [asymptotic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asymptotic_analysis "Asymptotic analysis") to ⁠x/log⁡x{\displaystyle x/\log x}![Image 32: {\displaystyle x/\log x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dcc3d89d9a592249b29d9ec90ec429423fc3db)⁠, where log⁡x{\displaystyle \log x}![Image 33: {\displaystyle \log x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/80d453de713a8c45f7bf99108752531ed7d6dd05) is the [natural logarithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_logarithm "Natural logarithm") of ⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 34: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠. A weaker consequence of this high density of primes was [Bertrand's postulate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bertrand%27s_postulate "Bertrand's postulate"), that for every n>1{\displaystyle n>1}![Image 35: {\displaystyle n>1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ee74e1cc07e7041edf0fcbd4481f5cd32ad17b64) there is a prime between ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 36: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ and ⁠2 n{\displaystyle 2n}![Image 37: {\displaystyle 2n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/134afa8ff09fdddd24b06f289e92e3a045092bd1)⁠, proved in 1852 by [Pafnuty Chebyshev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pafnuty_Chebyshev "Pafnuty Chebyshev").[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-24) Ideas of [Bernhard Riemann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernhard_Riemann "Bernhard Riemann") in his [1859 paper on the zeta-function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On_the_Number_of_Primes_Less_Than_a_Given_Magnitude "On the Number of Primes Less Than a Given Magnitude") sketched an outline for proving the conjecture of Legendre and Gauss. Although the closely related [Riemann hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_hypothesis "Riemann hypothesis") remains unproven, Riemann's outline was completed in 1896 by [Hadamard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacques_Hadamard "Jacques Hadamard") and [de la Vallée Poussin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Jean_de_la_Vall%C3%A9e-Poussin "Charles Jean de la Vallée-Poussin"), and the result is now known as the [prime number theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number_theorem "Prime number theorem").[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-25) Another important 19th century result was [Dirichlet's theorem on arithmetic progressions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirichlet%27s_theorem_on_arithmetic_progressions "Dirichlet's theorem on arithmetic progressions"), that certain [arithmetic progressions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic_progression "Arithmetic progression") contain infinitely many primes.[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-26)

Many mathematicians have worked on [primality tests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primality_test "Primality test") for numbers larger than those where trial division is practicably applicable. Methods that are restricted to specific number forms include [Pépin's test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/P%C3%A9pin%27s_test "Pépin's test") for Fermat numbers (1877),[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-27)[Proth's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proth%27s_theorem "Proth's theorem") (c. 1878),[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-28) the [Lucas–Lehmer primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucas%E2%80%93Lehmer_primality_test "Lucas–Lehmer primality test") (originated 1856), and the generalized [Lucas primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucas_primality_test "Lucas primality test").[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-mollin-17)

Since 1951 all the [largest known primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Largest_known_prime "Largest known prime") have been found using these tests on [computers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer "Computer").[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-30) The search for ever larger primes has generated interest outside mathematical circles, through the [Great Internet Mersenne Prime Search](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Internet_Mersenne_Prime_Search "Great Internet Mersenne Prime Search") and other [distributed computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Distributed_computing "Distributed computing") projects.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-ziegler-9)[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-31) The idea that prime numbers had few applications outside of [pure mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pure_mathematics "Pure mathematics")[[b]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-pure-34) was shattered in the 1970s when [public-key cryptography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public-key_cryptography "Public-key cryptography") and the [RSA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RSA_(cryptosystem) "RSA (cryptosystem)") cryptosystem were invented, using prime numbers as their basis.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-ent-7-35)

The increased practical importance of computerized primality testing and factorization led to the development of improved methods capable of handling large numbers of unrestricted form.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-pomerance-sciam-16)[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-36)[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-37) The mathematical theory of prime numbers also moved forward with the [Green–Tao theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green%E2%80%93Tao_theorem "Green–Tao theorem") (2004) that there are arbitrarily long arithmetic progressions of prime numbers, and [Yitang Zhang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yitang_Zhang "Yitang Zhang")'s 2013 proof that there exist infinitely many [prime gaps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_gap "Prime gap") of bounded size.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-neale-18-47-38)

### Primality of one

Most early Greeks did not even consider 1 to be a number,[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-crxk-34-39)[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-40) so they could not consider its primality. A few scholars in the Greek and later Roman tradition, including [Nicomachus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicomachus "Nicomachus"), [Iamblichus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iamblichus "Iamblichus"), [Boethius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boethius "Boethius"), and [Cassiodorus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cassiodorus "Cassiodorus"), also considered the prime numbers to be a subdivision of the odd numbers, so they did not consider ⁠2{\displaystyle 2}![Image 38: {\displaystyle 2}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/901fc910c19990d0dbaaefe4726ceb1a4e217a0f)⁠ to be prime either. However, Euclid and a majority of the other Greek mathematicians considered ⁠2{\displaystyle 2}![Image 39: {\displaystyle 2}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/901fc910c19990d0dbaaefe4726ceb1a4e217a0f)⁠ as prime. The [medieval Islamic mathematicians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam "Mathematics in medieval Islam") largely followed the Greeks in viewing 1 as not being a number.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-crxk-34-39) By the Middle Ages and Renaissance, mathematicians began treating 1 as a number, and by the 17th century some of them included it as the first prime number.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-41) In the mid-18th century, [Christian Goldbach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Goldbach "Christian Goldbach") listed 1 as prime in his correspondence with [Leonhard Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler "Leonhard Euler");[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-42) however, Euler himself did not consider 1 to be prime.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-43) Many 19th century mathematicians still considered 1 to be prime,[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-cx-44) and [Derrick Norman Lehmer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derrick_Norman_Lehmer "Derrick Norman Lehmer") included 1 in his _list of primes less than ten million_ published in 1914.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConwayGuy1996130-45) Lists of primes that included 1 continued to be published as recently as 1956.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-46)[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-cg-bon-129-130-47) However, by the early 20th century mathematicians began to agree that 1 should not be listed as prime, but rather in its own special category as a "[unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_(ring_theory) "Unit (ring theory)")".[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-cx-44)

If 1 were to be considered a prime, many statements involving primes would need to be awkwardly reworded. For example, the fundamental theorem of arithmetic would need to be rephrased in terms of factorizations into primes greater than 1, because every number would have multiple factorizations with any number of copies of 1.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-cx-44) Similarly, the [sieve of Eratosthenes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sieve_of_Eratosthenes "Sieve of Eratosthenes") would not work correctly if it handled 1 as a prime, because it would eliminate all multiples of 1 (that is, all other numbers) and output only the single number 1.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-cg-bon-129-130-47) Some other more technical properties of prime numbers also do not hold for the number 1: for instance, the formulas for [Euler's totient function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler%27s_totient_function "Euler's totient function") or for the [sum of divisors function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sum-of-divisors_function "Sum-of-divisors function") are different for prime numbers than they are for 1.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-48)

Elementary properties
---------------------

### Unique factorization

Main article: [Fundamental theorem of arithmetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_arithmetic "Fundamental theorem of arithmetic")

Writing a number as a product of prime numbers is called a _prime factorization_ of the number.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeff2000[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbarronsmathworkb00leff_0page64mode1upq22primefactorization22_64%E2%80%9365]-49) For example:

50=2×5×5=2×5 2.{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}50&=2\times 5\times 5\\&=2\times 5^{2}.\end{aligned}}}![Image 40: {\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}50&=2\times 5\times 5\\&=2\times 5^{2}.\end{aligned}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/027ed6ef8162c47c25e9dc13c0aefa473d153a7a)
The terms in the product are called _prime factors_. The same prime factor may occur more than once; this example has two copies of the prime factor 5.{\displaystyle 5.}![Image 41: {\displaystyle 5.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/62cfda82378d02d6ff65a09e66873314c7013888) When a prime occurs multiple times, [exponentiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exponentiation "Exponentiation") can be used to group together multiple copies of the same prime number: for example, in the second way of writing the product above, 5 2{\displaystyle 5^{2}}![Image 42: {\displaystyle 5^{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1ac42c99ce3b9abb17b955f9995dd37ba270905f) denotes the [square](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_(algebra) "Square (algebra)") or second power of ⁠>5{\displaystyle >5}![Image 43: {\displaystyle >5}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f2c1ce19b051b41f0b21aad1365e6998a614e07d)⁠.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeff2000[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbarronsmathworkb00leff_0page64mode1upq22primefactorization22_64%E2%80%9365]-49)

The central importance of prime numbers to number theory and mathematics in general stems from the _fundamental theorem of arithmetic_.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-50) This theorem states that every integer larger than 1 can be written as a product of one or more primes. More strongly, this product is unique in the sense that any two prime factorizations of the same number will have the same numbers of copies of the same primes, although their ordering may differ.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-51) So, although there are many different ways of finding a factorization using an [integer factorization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer_factorization "Integer factorization") algorithm, they all must produce the same result. Primes can thus be considered the "basic building blocks" of the natural numbers.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-52)

Some proofs of the uniqueness of prime factorizations are based on [Euclid's lemma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid%27s_lemma "Euclid's lemma"): If ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 44: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ is a prime number and ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 45: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ divides a product a b{\displaystyle ab}![Image 46: {\displaystyle ab}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/49337c5cf256196e2292f7047cb5da68c24ca95d) of integers ⁠a{\displaystyle a}![Image 47: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc)⁠ and b,{\displaystyle b,}![Image 48: {\displaystyle b,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bdb96677ba71b937617ca8751955f884f6306b64) then ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 49: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ divides ⁠a{\displaystyle a}![Image 50: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc)⁠ or ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 51: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ divides ⁠b{\displaystyle b}![Image 52: {\displaystyle b}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f11423fbb2e967f986e36804a8ae4271734917c3)⁠ (or both).[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-53) Conversely, if a number ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 53: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ has the property that when it divides a product it always divides at least one factor of the product, then ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 54: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ must be prime.[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-54)

### Infinitude

Main article: [Euclid's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid%27s_theorem "Euclid's theorem")

There are [infinitely](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinitely "Infinitely") many prime numbers. Another way of saying this is that the sequence

2,3,5,7,11,13,...{\displaystyle 2,3,5,7,11,13,...}![Image 55: {\displaystyle 2,3,5,7,11,13,...}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/550507cdac98fae90795d0cce3da12d627e9bb91)
of prime numbers never ends. This statement is referred to as _Euclid's theorem_ in honor of the ancient Greek mathematician [Euclid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid "Euclid"), since the first known proof for this statement is attributed to him. Many more proofs of the infinitude of primes are known, including an [analytical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis "Mathematical analysis") proof by [Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler "Euler"), [Goldbach's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Goldbach "Christian Goldbach")[proof](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat_number#Basic_properties "Fermat number") based on [Fermat numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat_number "Fermat number"),[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-55)[Furstenberg's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hillel_Furstenberg "Hillel Furstenberg")[proof using general topology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Furstenberg%27s_proof_of_the_infinitude_of_primes "Furstenberg's proof of the infinitude of primes"),[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-56) and [Kummer's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Kummer "Ernst Kummer") elegant proof.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-57)

[Euclid's proof](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid%27s_theorem "Euclid's theorem")[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-58) shows that every [finite list](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finite_set "Finite set") of primes is incomplete. The key idea is to multiply together the primes in any given list and add 1.{\displaystyle 1.}![Image 56: {\displaystyle 1.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/af8c4e445819b13a052647aa3eb2be990b0a4b24) If the list consists of the primes p 1,p 2,…,p n,{\displaystyle p_{1},p_{2},\ldots ,p_{n},}![Image 57: {\displaystyle p_{1},p_{2},\ldots ,p_{n},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5f1e3bbaf2b32c0dc5e79c1957b04b108bd09d71) this gives the number

N=1+p 1⋅p 2⋯p n.{\displaystyle N=1+p_{1}\cdot p_{2}\cdots p_{n}.}![Image 58: {\displaystyle N=1+p_{1}\cdot p_{2}\cdots p_{n}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fd1d057bffd1dbbc94420d3a0b32703cc3205f29)
By the fundamental theorem, ⁠N{\displaystyle N}![Image 59: {\displaystyle N}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5e3890c981ae85503089652feb48b191b57aae3)⁠ has a prime factorization

N=p 1′⋅p 2′⋯p m′{\displaystyle N=p'_{1}\cdot p'_{2}\cdots p'_{m}}![Image 60: {\displaystyle N=p'_{1}\cdot p'_{2}\cdots p'_{m}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/606c652811e0772e18cccc88c216bb3647958d53)
with one or more prime factors. ⁠N{\displaystyle N}![Image 61: {\displaystyle N}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5e3890c981ae85503089652feb48b191b57aae3)⁠ is evenly divisible by each of these factors, but ⁠N{\displaystyle N}![Image 62: {\displaystyle N}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5e3890c981ae85503089652feb48b191b57aae3)⁠ has a remainder of one when divided by any of the prime numbers in the given list, so none of the prime factors of ⁠N{\displaystyle N}![Image 63: {\displaystyle N}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5e3890c981ae85503089652feb48b191b57aae3)⁠ can be in the given list. Because there is no finite list of all the primes, there must be infinitely many primes.

The numbers formed by adding one to the products of the smallest primes are called [Euclid numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid_number "Euclid number").[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-59) The first five of them are prime, but the sixth,

1+(2⋅3⋅5⋅7⋅11⋅13)=30031=59⋅509,{\displaystyle 1+{\big (}2\cdot 3\cdot 5\cdot 7\cdot 11\cdot 13{\big )}=30031=59\cdot 509,}![Image 64: {\displaystyle 1+{\big (}2\cdot 3\cdot 5\cdot 7\cdot 11\cdot 13{\big )}=30031=59\cdot 509,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c24968a289b6e1411be6a5825a10543603d4613b)
is a composite number.

### Formulas for primes

Main article: [Formula for primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formula_for_primes "Formula for primes")

There is no known efficient formula for primes. For example, there is no non-constant [polynomial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polynomial "Polynomial"), even in several variables, that takes _only_ prime values.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-matiyasevich-60) However, there are numerous expressions that do encode all primes, or only primes. One possible formula is based on [Wilson's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilson%27s_theorem "Wilson's theorem") and generates the number 2 many times and all other primes exactly once.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-61) There is also a set of [Diophantine equations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diophantine_equations "Diophantine equations") in nine variables and one parameter with the following property: the parameter is prime if and only if the resulting system of equations has a solution over the natural numbers. This can be used to obtain a single formula with the property that all its _positive_ values are prime.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-matiyasevich-60)

Other examples of prime-generating formulas come from [Mills' theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mills%27_theorem "Mills' theorem") and a theorem of [Wright](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E._M._Wright "E. M. Wright"). These assert that there are real constants A>1{\displaystyle A>1}![Image 65: {\displaystyle A>1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f763115cc85f7fd1d4ffd590bdc135c74a58afec) and μ{\displaystyle \mu }![Image 66: {\displaystyle \mu }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fd47b2a39f7a7856952afec1f1db72c67af6161) such that

⌊A 3 n⌋and⌊2⋯2 2 μ⌋{\displaystyle \left\lfloor A^{3^{n}}\right\rfloor {\text{ and }}\left\lfloor 2^{\cdots ^{2^{2^{\mu }}}}\right\rfloor }![Image 67: {\displaystyle \left\lfloor A^{3^{n}}\right\rfloor {\text{ and }}\left\lfloor 2^{\cdots ^{2^{2^{\mu }}}}\right\rfloor }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c6695fd076eb01dd676dbadefc5375fe802d7355)
are prime for any natural number ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 68: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ in the first formula, and any number of exponents in the second formula.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-62) Here ⌊⋅⌋{\displaystyle \lfloor {}\cdot {}\rfloor }![Image 69: {\displaystyle \lfloor {}\cdot {}\rfloor }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a5a17bb92075611951d0ca47b9ab7876515c2507) represents the [floor function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Floor_function "Floor function"), the largest integer less than or equal to the number in question. However, these are not useful for generating primes, as the primes must be generated first in order to compute the values of ⁠A{\displaystyle A}![Image 70: {\displaystyle A}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7daff47fa58cdfd29dc333def748ff5fa4c923e3)⁠ or μ.{\displaystyle \mu .}![Image 71: {\displaystyle \mu .}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a1ef6db045c1f6193799bd25a4b68ba9f78646d2)[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-matiyasevich-60)

### Open questions

Further information: [Category:Conjectures about prime numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Category:Conjectures_about_prime_numbers "Category:Conjectures about prime numbers")

Many conjectures revolving about primes have been posed. Often having an elementary formulation, many of these conjectures have withstood proof for decades: all four of [Landau's problems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landau%27s_problems "Landau's problems") from 1912 are still unsolved.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-63) One of them is [Goldbach's conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goldbach%27s_conjecture "Goldbach's conjecture"), which asserts that every even integer ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 72: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ greater than ⁠2{\displaystyle 2}![Image 73: {\displaystyle 2}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/901fc910c19990d0dbaaefe4726ceb1a4e217a0f)⁠ can be written as a sum of two primes.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-64) As of 2014[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Prime_number&action=edit), this conjecture has been verified for all numbers up to n=4⋅10 18.{\displaystyle n=4\cdot 10^{18}.}![Image 74: {\displaystyle n=4\cdot 10^{18}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/27c89cde8425a207680113a97590e763bdbf5126)[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-65) Weaker statements than this have been proven; for example, [Vinogradov's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vinogradov%27s_theorem "Vinogradov's theorem") says that every sufficiently large odd integer can be written as a sum of three primes.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-66)[Chen's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chen%27s_theorem "Chen's theorem") says that every sufficiently large even number can be expressed as the sum of a prime and a [semiprime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semiprime "Semiprime") (the product of two primes).[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-67) Also, any even integer greater than 10 can be written as the sum of six primes.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-68) The branch of number theory studying such questions is called [additive number theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Additive_number_theory "Additive number theory").[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-69)

Another type of problem concerns [prime gaps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_gap "Prime gap"), the differences between consecutive primes. The existence of arbitrarily large prime gaps can be seen by noting that the sequence n!+2,n!+3,…,n!+n{\displaystyle n!+2,n!+3,\dots ,n!+n}![Image 75: {\displaystyle n!+2,n!+3,\dots ,n!+n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cf39c77f9ee05de53b4ae8bcf69ab8e5bbc0d119) consists of n−1{\displaystyle n-1}![Image 76: {\displaystyle n-1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fbd0b0f32b28f51962943ee9ede4fb34198a2521) composite numbers, for any natural number n.{\displaystyle n.}![Image 77: {\displaystyle n.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e59df02a9f67a5da3c220f1244c99a46cc4eb1c6)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-70) However, large prime gaps occur much earlier than this argument shows.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-riesel-gaps-71) For example, the first prime gap of length 8 is between the primes 89 and 97,[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-72) much smaller than 8!=40320.{\displaystyle 8!=40320.}![Image 78: {\displaystyle 8!=40320.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/76794af19af9e0c06865ed95d9a57e594a2e9bbf) It is conjectured that there are infinitely many [twin primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twin_prime "Twin prime"), pairs of primes with difference 2; this is the [twin prime conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twin_prime_conjecture "Twin prime conjecture"). [Polignac's conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polignac%27s_conjecture "Polignac's conjecture") states more generally that for every positive integer k,{\displaystyle k,}![Image 79: {\displaystyle k,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/44e185ab9c990830d5055fa3ae698a4225ce67e0) there are infinitely many pairs of consecutive primes that differ by 2 k.{\displaystyle 2k.}![Image 80: {\displaystyle 2k.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/77f52ba9e75bf2fb39818de19d98af82aafc4d67)[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-rib-gaps-73)[Andrica's conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrica%27s_conjecture "Andrica's conjecture"),[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-rib-gaps-73)[Brocard's conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brocard%27s_conjecture "Brocard's conjecture"),[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-rib-183-74)[Legendre's conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Legendre%27s_conjecture "Legendre's conjecture"),[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-chan-75) and [Oppermann's conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oppermann%27s_conjecture "Oppermann's conjecture")[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-rib-183-74) all suggest that the largest gaps between primes from 1 to ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 81: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ should be at most approximately n,{\displaystyle {\sqrt {n}},}![Image 82: {\displaystyle {\sqrt {n}},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a7173717a40b3c0819656acd987cacbc2feaf533) a result that is known to follow from the Riemann hypothesis, while the much stronger [Cramér conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cram%C3%A9r_conjecture "Cramér conjecture") sets the largest gap size at ⁠O((log⁡n)2){\displaystyle O((\log n)^{2})}![Image 83: {\displaystyle O((\log n)^{2})}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cbaf92b0d7a525c08aabd077e01c00d356581e65)⁠.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-rib-gaps-73) Prime gaps can be generalized to [prime ⁠k{\displaystyle k}![Image 84: {\displaystyle k}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40)⁠-tuples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_k-tuple "Prime k-tuple"), patterns in the differences among more than two prime numbers. Their infinitude and density are the subject of the [first Hardy–Littlewood conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Hardy%E2%80%93Littlewood_conjecture "First Hardy–Littlewood conjecture"), which can be motivated by the [heuristic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heuristic "Heuristic") that the prime numbers behave similarly to a random sequence of numbers with density given by the prime number theorem.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-76)

Analytic properties
-------------------

[Analytic number theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Analytic_number_theory "Analytic number theory") studies number theory through the lens of [continuous functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_function "Continuous function"), [limits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limit_(mathematics) "Limit (mathematics)"), [infinite series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Series_(mathematics) "Series (mathematics)"), and the related mathematics of the infinite and [infinitesimal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinitesimal "Infinitesimal").

This area of study began with [Leonhard Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler "Leonhard Euler") and his first major result, the solution to the [Basel problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basel_problem "Basel problem"). The problem asked for the value of the infinite sum 1+1 4+1 9+1 16+…,{\displaystyle 1+{\tfrac {1}{4}}+{\tfrac {1}{9}}+{\tfrac {1}{16}}+\dots ,}![Image 85: {\displaystyle 1+{\tfrac {1}{4}}+{\tfrac {1}{9}}+{\tfrac {1}{16}}+\dots ,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/879448d9d12ad037b8fde0be5082844a3da6ae09) which today can be recognized as the value ζ(2){\displaystyle \zeta (2)}![Image 86: {\displaystyle \zeta (2)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eff246e5aba5259593186618c576a3b7e14bc3c8) of the [Riemann zeta function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function "Riemann zeta function"). This function is closely connected to the prime numbers and to one of the most significant unsolved problems in mathematics, the [Riemann hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_hypothesis "Riemann hypothesis"). Euler showed that ⁠ζ(2)=π 2/6{\displaystyle \zeta (2)=\pi ^{2}/6}![Image 87: {\displaystyle \zeta (2)=\pi ^{2}/6}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/780b5b024e5d868b7e89ce95dd4cb682919623f4)⁠.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-77) The reciprocal of this number, ⁠6/π 2{\displaystyle 6/\pi ^{2}}![Image 88: {\displaystyle 6/\pi ^{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c4a29a93991c7d0dfd47e0c5b4ea3468fc2e41f7)⁠, is the limiting probability that two random numbers selected uniformly from a large range are [relatively prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coprime_integers "Coprime integers") (have no factors in common).[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-78)

The distribution of primes in the large, such as the question how many primes are smaller than a given, large threshold, is described by the [prime number theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number_theorem "Prime number theorem"), but no efficient [formula for the ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 89: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠-th prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formula_for_primes "Formula for primes") is known. [Dirichlet's theorem on arithmetic progressions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirichlet%27s_theorem_on_arithmetic_progressions "Dirichlet's theorem on arithmetic progressions"), in its basic form, asserts that linear polynomials

p(n)=a+b n{\displaystyle p(n)=a+bn}![Image 90: {\displaystyle p(n)=a+bn}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a9ec0a7d51df349db548143b777ac3a56f88d8a3)
with relatively prime integers ⁠a{\displaystyle a}![Image 91: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc)⁠ and ⁠b{\displaystyle b}![Image 92: {\displaystyle b}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f11423fbb2e967f986e36804a8ae4271734917c3)⁠ take infinitely many prime values. Stronger forms of the theorem state that the sum of the reciprocals of these prime values diverges, and that different linear polynomials with the same ⁠b{\displaystyle b}![Image 93: {\displaystyle b}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f11423fbb2e967f986e36804a8ae4271734917c3)⁠ have approximately the same proportions of primes. Although conjectures have been formulated about the proportions of primes in higher-degree polynomials, they remain unproven, and it is unknown whether there exists a quadratic polynomial that (for integer arguments) is prime infinitely often.

### Analytical proof of Euclid's theorem

[Euler's proof that there are infinitely many primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divergence_of_the_sum_of_the_reciprocals_of_the_primes "Divergence of the sum of the reciprocals of the primes") considers the sums of [reciprocals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiplicative_inverse "Multiplicative inverse") of primes,

1 2+1 3+1 5+1 7+⋯+1 p.{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{2}}+{\frac {1}{3}}+{\frac {1}{5}}+{\frac {1}{7}}+\cdots +{\frac {1}{p}}.}![Image 94: {\displaystyle {\frac {1}{2}}+{\frac {1}{3}}+{\frac {1}{5}}+{\frac {1}{7}}+\cdots +{\frac {1}{p}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/398379c591d6687ff6a128f25a7cd2fd3b20cfc3)
Euler showed that, for any arbitrary [real number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number "Real number")⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 95: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠, there exists a prime ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 96: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ for which this sum is greater than ⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 97: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-79) This shows that there are infinitely many primes, because if there were finitely many primes the sum would reach its maximum value at the biggest prime rather than growing past every⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 98: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠. The growth rate of this sum is described more precisely by [Mertens' second theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mertens%27_theorems "Mertens' theorems").[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-80) For comparison, the sum

1 1 2+1 2 2+1 3 2+⋯+1 n 2{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\cdots +{\frac {1}{n^{2}}}}![Image 99: {\displaystyle {\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\cdots +{\frac {1}{n^{2}}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d7be4306447ca3c94aa41f5683e7372ef912b72a)
does not grow to infinity as ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 100: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ goes to infinity (see the [Basel problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basel_problem "Basel problem")). In this sense, prime numbers occur more often than squares of natural numbers, although both sets are infinite.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-mtb-invitation-81)[Brun's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brun%27s_theorem "Brun's theorem") states that the sum of the reciprocals of [twin primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twin_prime "Twin prime"),

(1 3+1 5)+(1 5+1 7)+(1 11+1 13)+⋯,{\displaystyle \left({{\frac {1}{3}}+{\frac {1}{5}}}\right)+\left({{\frac {1}{5}}+{\frac {1}{7}}}\right)+\left({{\frac {1}{11}}+{\frac {1}{13}}}\right)+\cdots ,}![Image 101: {\displaystyle \left({{\frac {1}{3}}+{\frac {1}{5}}}\right)+\left({{\frac {1}{5}}+{\frac {1}{7}}}\right)+\left({{\frac {1}{11}}+{\frac {1}{13}}}\right)+\cdots ,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/61dd979f913d0f2173e98f7d4095909780bd6977)
is finite. Because of Brun's theorem, it is not possible to use Euler's method to solve the [twin prime conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twin_prime_conjecture "Twin prime conjecture"), that there exist infinitely many twin primes.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-mtb-invitation-81)

### Number of primes below a given bound

Main articles: [Prime number theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number_theorem "Prime number theorem") and [Prime-counting function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime-counting_function "Prime-counting function")

[![Image 102](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Prime-counting_relative_error.svg/500px-Prime-counting_relative_error.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Prime-counting_relative_error.svg)

The [relative error](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Approximation_error "Approximation error") of n log⁡n{\displaystyle {\tfrac {n}{\log n}}}![Image 103: {\displaystyle {\tfrac {n}{\log n}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/942db377004f15abd6b11d4d5487c436a2249d50) and the logarithmic integral Li⁡(n){\displaystyle \operatorname {Li} (n)}![Image 104: {\displaystyle \operatorname {Li} (n)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/61c711c5eda9013c9c1d65e69f35f05b99f03629) as approximations to the [prime-counting function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime-counting_function "Prime-counting function"). Both relative errors decrease to zero as ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 105: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ grows, but the convergence to zero is much more rapid for the logarithmic integral.

The [prime-counting function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime-counting_function "Prime-counting function")π(n){\displaystyle \pi (n)}![Image 106: {\displaystyle \pi (n)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac42d38c71b368d5fbf1e05753e9c5c038cd671b) is defined as the number of primes not greater than ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 107: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-82) For example, ⁠π(11)=5{\displaystyle \pi (11)=5}![Image 108: {\displaystyle \pi (11)=5}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f37a1ec8c22561cae405286228241267bec24e9f)⁠, since there are five primes less than or equal to 11. Methods such as the [Meissel–Lehmer algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meissel%E2%80%93Lehmer_algorithm "Meissel–Lehmer algorithm") can compute exact values of π(n){\displaystyle \pi (n)}![Image 109: {\displaystyle \pi (n)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac42d38c71b368d5fbf1e05753e9c5c038cd671b) faster than it would be possible to list each prime up to ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 110: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-83) The [prime number theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number_theorem "Prime number theorem") states that π(n){\displaystyle \pi (n)}![Image 111: {\displaystyle \pi (n)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac42d38c71b368d5fbf1e05753e9c5c038cd671b) is asymptotic to ⁠n/log⁡n{\displaystyle n/\log n}![Image 112: {\displaystyle n/\log n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dcf375088f1428c429e63be39e7852e8b39c77c0)⁠, which is denoted as

π(n)∼n log⁡n,{\displaystyle \pi (n)\sim {\frac {n}{\log n}},}![Image 113: {\displaystyle \pi (n)\sim {\frac {n}{\log n}},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/408b397046af4ade735aebb8995aa70ac2244838)
and means that the ratio of π(n){\displaystyle \pi (n)}![Image 114: {\displaystyle \pi (n)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac42d38c71b368d5fbf1e05753e9c5c038cd671b) to the right-hand fraction [approaches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_sequence "Convergent sequence") 1 as ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 115: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ grows to infinity.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-cranpom10-84) This implies that the likelihood that a randomly chosen number less than ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 116: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ is prime is (approximately) inversely proportional to the number of digits in⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 117: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-85) It also implies that the ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 118: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠th prime number is proportional to n log⁡n{\displaystyle n\log n}![Image 119: {\displaystyle n\log n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/560dfdce0353a330e03e4b3e0b7ca6e484bb40fb)[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-86) and therefore that the average size of a prime gap is proportional to ⁠log⁡n{\displaystyle \log n}![Image 120: {\displaystyle \log n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/317ab5292da7c7935aec01a570461fe0613b21d5)⁠.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-riesel-gaps-71) A more accurate estimate for π(n){\displaystyle \pi (n)}![Image 121: {\displaystyle \pi (n)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac42d38c71b368d5fbf1e05753e9c5c038cd671b) is given by the [offset logarithmic integral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Offset_logarithmic_integral "Offset logarithmic integral")[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-cranpom10-84)

π(n)∼Li⁡(n)=∫2 n d t log⁡t.{\displaystyle \pi (n)\sim \operatorname {Li} (n)=\int _{2}^{n}{\frac {dt}{\log t}}.}![Image 122: {\displaystyle \pi (n)\sim \operatorname {Li} (n)=\int _{2}^{n}{\frac {dt}{\log t}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f6b5a9dac3f86b36f631c01ed4aae859cdedf79c)

### Arithmetic progressions

Main articles: [Dirichlet's theorem on arithmetic progressions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirichlet%27s_theorem_on_arithmetic_progressions "Dirichlet's theorem on arithmetic progressions") and [Green–Tao theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green%E2%80%93Tao_theorem "Green–Tao theorem")

An [arithmetic progression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic_progression "Arithmetic progression") is a finite or infinite sequence of numbers such that consecutive numbers in the sequence all have the same difference.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-87) This difference is called the [modulus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_arithmetic "Modular arithmetic") of the progression.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-88) For example,

3,12,21,30,39,...,{\displaystyle 3,12,21,30,39,...,}![Image 123: {\displaystyle 3,12,21,30,39,...,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dba6b3978ece6a0b4c8fb4cc97124bc861ba8599)
is an infinite arithmetic progression with modulus 9. In an arithmetic progression, all the numbers have the same remainder when divided by the modulus; in this example, the remainder is 3. Because both the modulus 9 and the remainder 3 are multiples of 3, so is every element in the sequence. Therefore, this progression contains only one prime number, 3 itself. In general, the infinite progression

a,a+q,a+2 q,a+3 q,…{\displaystyle a,a+q,a+2q,a+3q,\dots }![Image 124: {\displaystyle a,a+q,a+2q,a+3q,\dots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/59d16a7879ded2fe4112aae7ab945259fc40eda8)
can have more than one prime only when its remainder ⁠a{\displaystyle a}![Image 125: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc)⁠ and modulus ⁠q{\displaystyle q}![Image 126: {\displaystyle q}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/06809d64fa7c817ffc7e323f85997f783dbdf71d)⁠ are relatively prime. If they are relatively prime, [Dirichlet's theorem on arithmetic progressions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirichlet%27s_theorem_on_arithmetic_progressions "Dirichlet's theorem on arithmetic progressions") asserts that the progression contains infinitely many primes.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-89)

[![Image 127: Prime numbers in arithmetic progression mod 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/Prime_numbers_in_arithmetic_progression_mod_9_zoom_in.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Prime_numbers_in_arithmetic_progression_mod_9_zoom_in.png "Primes in the arithmetic progressions modulo 9. Each row of the thin horizontal band shows one of the nine possible progressions mod 9, with prime numbers marked in red. The progressions of numbers that are 0, 3, or 6 mod 9 contain at most one prime number (the number 3); the remaining progressions of numbers that are 2, 4, 5, 7, and 8 mod 9 have infinitely many prime numbers, with similar numbers of primes in each progression.")

[](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Prime_numbers_in_arithmetic_progression_mod_9_zoom_in.png "File:Prime numbers in arithmetic progression mod 9 zoom in.png")

Primes in the arithmetic progressions modulo 9. Each row of the thin horizontal band shows one of the nine possible progressions mod 9, with prime numbers marked in red. The progressions of numbers that are 0, 3, or 6 mod 9 contain at most one prime number (the number 3); the remaining progressions of numbers that are 2, 4, 5, 7, and 8 mod 9 have infinitely many prime numbers, with similar numbers of primes in each progression.

The [Green–Tao theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green%E2%80%93Tao_theorem "Green–Tao theorem") shows that there are arbitrarily long finite arithmetic progressions consisting only of primes.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-neale-18-47-38)[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-90)

### Prime values of quadratic polynomials

[![Image 128: The Ulam spiral](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Ulam_2.png/330px-Ulam_2.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ulam_2.png)

The [Ulam spiral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulam_spiral "Ulam spiral"). Prime numbers (orange) cluster on some diagonals and not others. Prime values of 4 n 2−2 n+41{\displaystyle 4n^{2}-2n+41}![Image 129: {\displaystyle 4n^{2}-2n+41}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/47f7b0b039e84f6a35cf179e74583d3217eced75) are shown in blue.

Euler noted that the function

n 2−n+41{\displaystyle n^{2}-n+41}![Image 130: {\displaystyle n^{2}-n+41}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c7c4504253486e5ff537490356c8529390687bdb)
yields prime numbers for ⁠1≤n≤40{\displaystyle 1\leq n\leq 40}![Image 131: {\displaystyle 1\leq n\leq 40}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c1d90b605eed00af6cd0590bb9591798ea4497f2)⁠, although composite numbers appear among its later values.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-91)[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-92) The search for an explanation for this phenomenon led to the deep [algebraic number theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_number_theory "Algebraic number theory") of [Heegner numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heegner_number "Heegner number") and the [class number problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_number_problem "Class number problem").[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-93) The [Hardy–Littlewood conjecture F](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hardy%E2%80%93Littlewood_conjecture_F "Hardy–Littlewood conjecture F") predicts the density of primes among the values of [quadratic polynomials](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_polynomial "Quadratic polynomial") with integer [coefficients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coefficient "Coefficient") in terms of the logarithmic integral and the polynomial coefficients. No quadratic polynomial has been proven to take infinitely many prime values.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-guy-a1-94)

The [Ulam spiral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulam_spiral "Ulam spiral")[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-95) arranges the natural numbers in a two-dimensional grid, spiraling in concentric squares surrounding the origin with the prime numbers highlighted. Visually, the primes appear to cluster on certain diagonals and not others, suggesting that some quadratic polynomials take prime values more often than others.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-guy-a1-94)

### Zeta function and the Riemann hypothesis

Main article: [Riemann hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_hypothesis "Riemann hypothesis")

[![Image 132: Plot of the absolute values of the zeta function](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Riemann_zeta_function_absolute_value.png/500px-Riemann_zeta_function_absolute_value.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Riemann_zeta_function_absolute_value.png)

Plot of the absolute values of the zeta function, showing some of its features

One of the most famous unsolved questions in mathematics, dating from 1859, and one of the [Millennium Prize Problems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millennium_Prize_Problems "Millennium Prize Problems"), is the [Riemann hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_hypothesis "Riemann hypothesis"), which asks where the [zeros](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zero_of_a_function "Zero of a function") of the [Riemann zeta function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function "Riemann zeta function")ζ(s){\displaystyle \zeta (s)}![Image 133: {\displaystyle \zeta (s)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cbd45922057e4d7a5718ce5ed703ab493c63897a) are located. This function is an [analytic function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Analytic_function "Analytic function") on the [complex numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number "Complex number"). For complex numbers ⁠s{\displaystyle s}![Image 134: {\displaystyle s}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/01d131dfd7673938b947072a13a9744fe997e632)⁠ with real part greater than one it equals both an [infinite sum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Series_(mathematics) "Series (mathematics)") over all integers, and an [infinite product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_product "Infinite product") over the prime numbers,

ζ(s)=∑n=1∞1 n s=∏p prime 1 1−p−s.{\displaystyle \zeta (s)=\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{n^{s}}}=\prod _{p{\text{ prime}}}{\frac {1}{1-p^{-s}}}.}![Image 135: {\displaystyle \zeta (s)=\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{n^{s}}}=\prod _{p{\text{ prime}}}{\frac {1}{1-p^{-s}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e6ee4dae49e217914ed83b6d9c27155a6eca1c3a)
This equality between a sum and a product, discovered by Euler, is called an [Euler product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler_product "Euler product").[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-96) The Euler product can be derived from the fundamental theorem of arithmetic, and shows the close connection between the zeta function and the prime numbers.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-97) It leads to another proof that there are infinitely many primes: if there were only finitely many, then the sum-product equality would also be valid at ⁠s=1{\displaystyle s=1}![Image 136: {\displaystyle s=1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bac386d8f227fb823cede9b3e33d706cad3ed306)⁠, but the sum would diverge (it is the [harmonic series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmonic_series_(mathematics) "Harmonic series (mathematics)")⁠1+1 2+1 3+…{\displaystyle 1+{\tfrac {1}{2}}+{\tfrac {1}{3}}+\dots }![Image 137: {\displaystyle 1+{\tfrac {1}{2}}+{\tfrac {1}{3}}+\dots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/391a7209d3509160364ad66fc270afc07d84c0db)⁠) while the product would be finite, a contradiction.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-98)

The Riemann hypothesis states that the [zeros](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zero_of_a_function "Zero of a function") of the zeta-function are all either negative even numbers, or complex numbers with [real part](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_part "Real part") equal to 1/2.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-99) The original proof of the [prime number theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number_theorem "Prime number theorem") was based on a weak form of this hypothesis, that there are no zeros with real part equal to 1,[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-100)[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-bcrw18-101) although other more elementary proofs have been found.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-102) The prime-counting function can be expressed by [Riemann's explicit formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann%27s_explicit_formula "Riemann's explicit formula") as a sum in which each term comes from one of the zeros of the zeta function; the main term of this sum is the logarithmic integral, and the remaining terms cause the sum to fluctuate above and below the main term.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-103) In this sense, the zeros control how regularly the prime numbers are distributed. If the Riemann hypothesis is true, these fluctuations will be small, and the [asymptotic distribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asymptotic_distribution "Asymptotic distribution") of primes given by the prime number theorem will also hold over much shorter intervals (of length about the square root of ⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 138: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠ for intervals near a number ⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 139: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠).[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-bcrw18-101)

Abstract algebra
----------------

### Modular arithmetic and finite fields

Main article: [Modular arithmetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_arithmetic "Modular arithmetic")

Modular arithmetic modifies usual arithmetic by only using the numbers ⁠{0,1,2,…,n−1}{\displaystyle \{0,1,2,\dots ,n-1\}}![Image 140: {\displaystyle \{0,1,2,\dots ,n-1\}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a274c78335c05b113ff9a19b66526a9da869fbfd)⁠, for a natural number ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 141: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ called the modulus. Any other natural number can be mapped into this system by replacing it by its remainder after division by ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 142: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-104) Modular sums, differences and products are calculated by performing the same replacement by the remainder on the result of the usual sum, difference, or product of integers.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-105) Equality of integers corresponds to _congruence_ in modular arithmetic: ⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 143: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠ and ⁠y{\displaystyle y}![Image 144: {\displaystyle y}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b8a6208ec717213d4317e666f1ae872e00620a0d)⁠ are congruent (written x≡y{\displaystyle x\equiv y}![Image 145: {\displaystyle x\equiv y}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/344d00ea5455ff5454015cc1f00cf534be450a03) mod ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 146: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠) when they have the same remainder after division by ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 147: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-106) However, in this system of numbers, [division](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Division_(mathematics) "Division (mathematics)") by all nonzero numbers is possible if and only if the modulus is prime.
