For instance, with the prime number 7 as modulus, division by 3 is possible: ⁠2/3≡3 mod 7{\displaystyle 2/3\equiv 3{\bmod {7}}}![Image 148: {\displaystyle 2/3\equiv 3{\bmod {7}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c7e6eb9bb6b2d861011df01e930241a2b1338af5)⁠, because [clearing denominators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clearing_denominators "Clearing denominators") by multiplying both sides by 3 gives the valid formula ⁠2≡9 mod 7{\displaystyle 2\equiv 9{\bmod {7}}}![Image 149: {\displaystyle 2\equiv 9{\bmod {7}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a9cc49f8e6ba7fbf81201a0a8d5b3ce614135853)⁠. However, with the composite modulus 6, division by 3 is impossible. There is no valid solution to 2/3≡x mod 6{\displaystyle 2/3\equiv x{\bmod {6}}}![Image 150: {\displaystyle 2/3\equiv x{\bmod {6}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/959d2e86e50ec85744347d794e18cc3d107979cd): clearing denominators by multiplying by 3 causes the left-hand side to become 2 while the right-hand side becomes either 0 or 3. In the terminology of [abstract algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstract_algebra "Abstract algebra"), the ability to perform division means that modular arithmetic modulo a prime number forms a [field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Field_(mathematics) "Field (mathematics)") or, more specifically, a [finite field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finite_field "Finite field"), while other moduli only give a [ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ring_(mathematics) "Ring (mathematics)") but not a field.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-107)

Several theorems about primes can be formulated using modular arithmetic. For instance, [Fermat's little theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat%27s_little_theorem "Fermat's little theorem") states that if a≢0{\displaystyle a\not \equiv 0}![Image 151: {\displaystyle a\not \equiv 0}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ff4a234827d107c1dd3ffc19005990ea92f4e4db) (mod ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 152: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠), then a p−1≡1{\displaystyle a^{p-1}\equiv 1}![Image 153: {\displaystyle a^{p-1}\equiv 1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d3d0c1282f62b25fc17d3ce40e36e3b2a2f541f1) (mod ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 154: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠).[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-108) Summing this over all choices of ⁠a{\displaystyle a}![Image 155: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc)⁠ gives the equation

∑a=1 p−1 a p−1≡(p−1)⋅1≡−1(mod p),{\displaystyle \sum _{a=1}^{p-1}a^{p-1}\equiv (p-1)\cdot 1\equiv -1{\pmod {p}},}![Image 156: {\displaystyle \sum _{a=1}^{p-1}a^{p-1}\equiv (p-1)\cdot 1\equiv -1{\pmod {p}},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4ac4572eadd7182309e72510091be6fe559e024f)
valid whenever ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 157: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ is prime. [Giuga's conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giuga%27s_conjecture "Giuga's conjecture") says that this equation is also a sufficient condition for ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 158: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ to be prime.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-109)[Wilson's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilson%27s_theorem "Wilson's theorem") says that an integer p>1{\displaystyle p>1}![Image 159: {\displaystyle p>1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0f127e7a5f2449ddf3edb8164c2d2439120641f9) is prime if and only if the [factorial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Factorial "Factorial")(p−1)!{\displaystyle (p-1)!}![Image 160: {\displaystyle (p-1)!}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ba68358eefe786c8448a04d3be5fa11279a59c62) is congruent to −1{\displaystyle -1}![Image 161: {\displaystyle -1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/704fb0427140d054dd267925495e78164fee9aac) mod ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 162: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠. For a composite number⁠n=r⋅s{\displaystyle n=r\cdot s}![Image 163: {\displaystyle n=r\cdot s}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/444e62edb2b45c9e4422a74ad3ceaf5c208f1e70)⁠ this cannot hold, since one of its factors divides both n and ⁠(n−1)!{\displaystyle (n-1)!}![Image 164: {\displaystyle (n-1)!}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7eb71a5f562b97650728f30493f43e96c15b4287)⁠, and so (n−1)!≡−1(mod n){\displaystyle (n-1)!\equiv -1{\pmod {n}}}![Image 165: {\displaystyle (n-1)!\equiv -1{\pmod {n}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5b29169bc8c822e9d212b319d1f610e0998d3fef) is impossible.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-110)

### _p_-adic numbers

Main article: [p-adic number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/P-adic_number "P-adic number")

The [⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 166: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠-adic order](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/P-adic_order "P-adic order")ν p(n){\displaystyle \nu _{p}(n)}![Image 167: {\displaystyle \nu _{p}(n)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a740c63898dac75666e43736eb3ae87eb8c62b16) of an integer ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 168: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ is the number of copies of ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 169: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ in the prime factorization of ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 170: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠. The same concept can be extended from integers to rational numbers by defining the ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 171: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠-adic order of a fraction m/n{\displaystyle m/n}![Image 172: {\displaystyle m/n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2eebcb27a9df80445dbe86eefee5d131d6e0e7e8) to be ⁠ν p(m)−ν p(n){\displaystyle \nu _{p}(m)-\nu _{p}(n)}![Image 173: {\displaystyle \nu _{p}(m)-\nu _{p}(n)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1d324584195f4488cae3200e33bcb93f605f43ed)⁠. The ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 174: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠-adic absolute value |q|p{\displaystyle |q|_{p}}![Image 175: {\displaystyle |q|_{p}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/699cfd1fc1d9b3543ebf4cb30e9d3a5a73a61bc9) of any rational number ⁠q{\displaystyle q}![Image 176: {\displaystyle q}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/06809d64fa7c817ffc7e323f85997f783dbdf71d)⁠ is then defined as ⁠|q|p=p−ν p(q){\displaystyle \vert q\vert _{p}=p^{-\nu _{p}(q)}}![Image 177: {\displaystyle \vert q\vert _{p}=p^{-\nu _{p}(q)}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6911fdcecc110622e53a5e3a08ab0f4412aa2e8a)⁠. Multiplying an integer by its ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 178: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠-adic absolute value cancels out the factors of ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 179: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ in its factorization, leaving only the other primes. Just as the distance between two real numbers can be measured by the absolute value of their distance, the distance between two rational numbers can be measured by their ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 180: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠-adic distance, the ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 181: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠-adic absolute value of their difference. For this definition of distance, two numbers are close together (they have a small distance) when their difference is divisible by a high power of ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 182: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠. In the same way that the real numbers can be formed from the rational numbers and their distances, by adding extra limiting values to form a [complete field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complete_field "Complete field"), the rational numbers with the ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 183: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠-adic distance can be extended to a different complete field, the [⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 184: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠-adic numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/P-adic_number "P-adic number").[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-childress-111)[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-112)

This picture of an order, absolute value, and complete field derived from them can be generalized to [algebraic number fields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_number_field "Algebraic number field") and their [valuations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valuation_(algebra) "Valuation (algebra)") (certain mappings from the [multiplicative group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiplicative_group "Multiplicative group") of the field to a [totally ordered additive group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Totally_ordered_group "Totally ordered group"), also called orders), [absolute values](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_value_(algebra) "Absolute value (algebra)") (certain multiplicative mappings from the field to the real numbers, also called [norms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norm_(mathematics) "Norm (mathematics)")),[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-childress-111) and places (extensions to [complete fields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complete_field "Complete field") in which the given field is a [dense set](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dense_set "Dense set"), also called completions).[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-113) The extension from the rational numbers to the [real numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number "Real number"), for instance, is a place in which the distance between numbers is the usual [absolute value](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_value "Absolute value") of their difference. The corresponding mapping to an additive group would be the [logarithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logarithm "Logarithm") of the absolute value, although this does not meet all the requirements of a valuation. According to [Ostrowski's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ostrowski%27s_theorem "Ostrowski's theorem"), up to a natural notion of equivalence, the real numbers and ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 185: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠-adic numbers, with their orders and absolute values, are the only valuations, absolute values, and places on the rational numbers.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-childress-111) The [local–global principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local%E2%80%93global_principle "Local–global principle") allows certain problems over the rational numbers to be solved by piecing together solutions from each of their places, again underlining the importance of primes to number theory.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-114)

### Prime elements of a ring

Main articles: [Prime element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_element "Prime element") and [Irreducible element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irreducible_element "Irreducible element")

[![Image 186](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Gaussian_primes.svg/250px-Gaussian_primes.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gaussian_primes.svg)

All [Gaussian primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_prime "Gaussian prime") with norm squared less than 500

A [commutative ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commutative_ring "Commutative ring") is an [algebraic structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_structure "Algebraic structure") where addition, subtraction and multiplication are defined. The integers are a ring, and the prime numbers in the integers have been generalized to rings in two different ways, _prime elements_ and _irreducible elements_. An element ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 187: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ of a ring ⁠R{\displaystyle R}![Image 188: {\displaystyle R}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b0bfb3769bf24d80e15374dc37b0441e2616e33)⁠ is called prime if it is nonzero, has no [multiplicative inverse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiplicative_inverse "Multiplicative inverse") (that is, it is not a [unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_(ring_theory) "Unit (ring theory)")), and satisfies the following requirement: whenever ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 189: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ divides the product x y{\displaystyle xy}![Image 190: {\displaystyle xy}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c72eb345e496513fb8b2fa4aa8c4d89b855f9a01) of two elements of ⁠R{\displaystyle R}![Image 191: {\displaystyle R}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b0bfb3769bf24d80e15374dc37b0441e2616e33)⁠, it also divides at least one of ⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 192: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠ or ⁠y{\displaystyle y}![Image 193: {\displaystyle y}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b8a6208ec717213d4317e666f1ae872e00620a0d)⁠. An element is irreducible if it is neither a unit nor the product of two other non-unit elements. In the ring of integers, the prime and irreducible elements form the same set,

{…,−11,−7,−5,−3,−2,2,3,5,7,11,…}.{\displaystyle \{\dots ,-11,-7,-5,-3,-2,2,3,5,7,11,\dots \}\,.}![Image 194: {\displaystyle \{\dots ,-11,-7,-5,-3,-2,2,3,5,7,11,\dots \}\,.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/521b9a30f8065b6659540de5e2d79f23b8ae8009)
In an arbitrary ring, all prime elements are irreducible. The converse does not hold in general, but does hold for [unique factorization domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unique_factorization_domain "Unique factorization domain").[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-115)

The fundamental theorem of arithmetic continues to hold (by definition) in unique factorization domains. An example of such a domain is the [Gaussian integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_integer "Gaussian integer")⁠Z[i]{\displaystyle \mathbb {Z} [i]}![Image 195: {\displaystyle \mathbb {Z} [i]}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9ffa94e9e2e6d9e5e5373d5fafb954b902743fde)⁠, the ring of [complex numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number "Complex number") of the form a+b i{\displaystyle a+bi}![Image 196: {\displaystyle a+bi}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a92f853c2c9235c06be640b91b7c75e2a907cbda) where ⁠i{\displaystyle i}![Image 197: {\displaystyle i}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/add78d8608ad86e54951b8c8bd6c8d8416533d20)⁠ denotes the [imaginary unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imaginary_unit "Imaginary unit") and ⁠a{\displaystyle a}![Image 198: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc)⁠ and ⁠b{\displaystyle b}![Image 199: {\displaystyle b}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f11423fbb2e967f986e36804a8ae4271734917c3)⁠ are arbitrary integers. Its prime elements are known as [Gaussian primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_prime "Gaussian prime"). Not every number that is prime among the integers remains prime in the Gaussian integers; for instance, the number 2 can be written as a product of the two Gaussian primes 1+i{\displaystyle 1+i}![Image 200: {\displaystyle 1+i}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1a65e41a5c0369e908cf26a2452046f19bab946d) and ⁠1−i{\displaystyle 1-i}![Image 201: {\displaystyle 1-i}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/685fed923493e7884e748f0dd6f932311f66bd69)⁠. Rational primes (the prime elements in the integers) congruent to 3 mod 4 are Gaussian primes, but rational primes congruent to 1 mod 4 are not.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-116) This is a consequence of [Fermat's theorem on sums of two squares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat%27s_theorem_on_sums_of_two_squares "Fermat's theorem on sums of two squares"), which states that an odd prime ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 202: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ is expressible as the sum of two squares, ⁠p=x 2+y 2{\displaystyle p=x^{2}+y^{2}}![Image 203: {\displaystyle p=x^{2}+y^{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fe433e9ba9c42efe1bc9fd51bb439c698b8f6843)⁠, and therefore factorable as ⁠p=(x+i y)(x−i y){\displaystyle p=(x+iy)(x-iy)}![Image 204: {\displaystyle p=(x+iy)(x-iy)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cba304bbcb900df2560ef81f194004d2cd365ddc)⁠, exactly when ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 205: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ is 1 mod 4.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-117)

### Prime ideals

Main article: [Prime ideals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_ideals "Prime ideals")

Not every ring is a unique factorization domain. For instance, in the ring of numbers a+b−5{\displaystyle a+b{\sqrt {-5}}}![Image 206: {\displaystyle a+b{\sqrt {-5}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5894d5cf1749ce9cb9be987542cabde8b3b8f2a5) (for integers ⁠a{\displaystyle a}![Image 207: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc)⁠ and ⁠b{\displaystyle b}![Image 208: {\displaystyle b}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f11423fbb2e967f986e36804a8ae4271734917c3)⁠) the number 21{\displaystyle 21}![Image 209: {\displaystyle 21}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/77ebb9ccf6080ba5c9a6ea8973cb2f26c50211cf) has two factorizations ⁠21=3⋅7=(1+2−5)(1−2−5){\displaystyle 21=3\cdot 7=(1+2{\sqrt {-5}})(1-2{\sqrt {-5}})}![Image 210: {\displaystyle 21=3\cdot 7=(1+2{\sqrt {-5}})(1-2{\sqrt {-5}})}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/85ef78d9e6efc73f20864dade3df3c2c9ae57f20)⁠, where none of the four factors can be reduced any further, so it does not have a unique factorization. In order to extend unique factorization to a larger class of rings, the notion of a number can be replaced with that of an [ideal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory) "Ideal (ring theory)"), a subset of the elements of a ring that contains all sums of pairs of its elements, and all products of its elements with ring elements. _Prime ideals_, which generalize prime elements in the sense that the [principal ideal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principal_ideal "Principal ideal") generated by a prime element is a prime ideal, are an important tool and object of study in [commutative algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commutative_algebra "Commutative algebra"), [algebraic number theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_theory "Number theory") and [algebraic geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_geometry "Algebraic geometry"). The prime ideals of the ring of integers are the ideals ⁠(0){\displaystyle (0)}![Image 211: {\displaystyle (0)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bf6d00d0d700cc9a652fb7e4b7f858b6eb2e9925)⁠, ⁠(2){\displaystyle (2)}![Image 212: {\displaystyle (2)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/43f88fdd4acbb57a291f9eb9f23ae23a1e492b30)⁠, ⁠(3){\displaystyle (3)}![Image 213: {\displaystyle (3)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a0ff45737214013a8e04d59d0de54318086be26a)⁠, ⁠(5){\displaystyle (5)}![Image 214: {\displaystyle (5)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6976219ec0cd50c8bd30b4a275b8f9780640c876)⁠, ⁠(7){\displaystyle (7)}![Image 215: {\displaystyle (7)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b9f80d74b83240744e88a7b1a091a974e987489b)⁠, ⁠(11){\displaystyle (11)}![Image 216: {\displaystyle (11)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/12c6a88867b3cfef93182c6579d1e4d74e1a46d3)⁠, ... The fundamental theorem of arithmetic generalizes to the [Lasker–Noether theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lasker%E2%80%93Noether_theorem "Lasker–Noether theorem"), which expresses every ideal in a [Noetherian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noetherian_ring "Noetherian ring")[commutative ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commutative_ring "Commutative ring") as an intersection of [primary ideals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primary_ideal "Primary ideal"), which are the appropriate generalizations of [prime powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_power "Prime power").[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-118)

The [spectrum of a ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectrum_of_a_ring "Spectrum of a ring") is a geometric space whose points are the prime ideals of the ring.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-119)[Arithmetic geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic_geometry "Arithmetic geometry") also benefits from this notion, and many concepts exist in both geometry and number theory. For example, factorization or [ramification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Splitting_of_prime_ideals_in_Galois_extensions "Splitting of prime ideals in Galois extensions") of prime ideals when lifted to an [extension field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Field_extension "Field extension"), a basic problem of algebraic number theory, bears some resemblance with [ramification in geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ramified_cover "Ramified cover"). These concepts can even assist with in number-theoretic questions solely concerned with integers. For example, prime ideals in the [ring of integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ring_of_integers "Ring of integers") of [quadratic number fields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_number_field "Quadratic number field") can be used in proving [quadratic reciprocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_reciprocity "Quadratic reciprocity"), a statement that concerns the existence of square roots modulo integer prime numbers.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-120) Early attempts to prove [Fermat's Last Theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat%27s_Last_Theorem "Fermat's Last Theorem") led to [Kummer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Kummer "Ernst Kummer")'s introduction of [regular primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_prime "Regular prime"), integer prime numbers connected with the failure of unique factorization in the [cyclotomic integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyclotomic_field "Cyclotomic field").[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-121) The question of how many integer prime numbers factor into a product of multiple prime ideals in an algebraic number field is addressed by [Chebotarev's density theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chebotarev%27s_density_theorem "Chebotarev's density theorem"), which (when applied to the cyclotomic integers) has Dirichlet's theorem on primes in arithmetic progressions as a special case.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-122)

### Group theory

In the theory of [finite groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finite_group "Finite group") the [Sylow theorems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sylow_theorems "Sylow theorems") imply that, if a power of a prime number p n{\displaystyle p^{n}}![Image 217: {\displaystyle p^{n}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c6a7a7e74ae90ab94f01e1629177758fb68b423b) divides the [order of a group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_of_a_group "Order of a group"), then the group has a subgroup of order ⁠p n{\displaystyle p^{n}}![Image 218: {\displaystyle p^{n}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c6a7a7e74ae90ab94f01e1629177758fb68b423b)⁠. By [Lagrange's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrange%27s_theorem_(group_theory) "Lagrange's theorem (group theory)"), any group of prime order is a [cyclic group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyclic_group "Cyclic group"), and by [Burnside's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burnside%27s_theorem "Burnside's theorem") any group whose order is divisible by only two primes is [solvable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solvable_group "Solvable group").[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-123)

Computational methods
---------------------

[![Image 219](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Gears_large.jpg/250px-Gears_large.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gears_large.jpg)

The small gear in this piece of farm equipment has 13 teeth, a prime number, and the middle gear has 21, relatively prime to 13.

For a long time, number theory in general, and the study of prime numbers in particular, was seen as the canonical example of pure mathematics, with no applications outside of mathematics[[b]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-pure-34) other than the use of prime numbered gear teeth to distribute wear evenly.[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-124) In particular, number theorists such as [British](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom "United Kingdom") mathematician [G. H. Hardy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G._H._Hardy "G. H. Hardy") prided themselves on doing work that had absolutely no military significance.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-125)

This vision of the purity of number theory was shattered in the 1970s, when it was publicly announced that prime numbers could be used as the basis for the creation of [public-key cryptography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public-key_cryptography "Public-key cryptography") algorithms.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-ent-7-35) These applications have led to significant study of [algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algorithm "Algorithm") for computing with prime numbers, and in particular of [primality testing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primality_test "Primality test"), methods for determining whether a given number is prime. The most basic primality testing routine, trial division, is too slow to be useful for large numbers. One group of modern primality tests is applicable to arbitrary numbers, while more efficient tests are available for numbers of special types. Most primality tests only tell whether their argument is prime or not. Routines that also provide a prime factor of composite arguments (or all of its prime factors) are called [factorization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer_factorization "Integer factorization") algorithms. Prime numbers are also used in computing for [checksums](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Checksum "Checksum"), [hash tables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hash_table "Hash table"), and [pseudorandom number generators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudorandom_number_generator "Pseudorandom number generator").

### Trial division

Main article: [Trial division](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trial_division "Trial division")

The most basic method of checking the primality of a given integer ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 220: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ is called _[trial division](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trial\_division "Trial division")_. This method divides ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 221: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ by each integer from 2 up to the [square root](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_root "Square root") of ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 222: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠. Any such integer dividing ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 223: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ evenly establishes ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 224: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ as composite; otherwise it is prime. Integers larger than the square root do not need to be checked because, whenever ⁠n=a⋅b{\displaystyle n=a\cdot b}![Image 225: {\displaystyle n=a\cdot b}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/083e9fcf193435c0829806a150b537e26641793c)⁠, one of the two factors ⁠a{\displaystyle a}![Image 226: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc)⁠ and ⁠b{\displaystyle b}![Image 227: {\displaystyle b}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f11423fbb2e967f986e36804a8ae4271734917c3)⁠ is less than or equal to the [square root](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_root "Square root") of ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 228: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠. Another optimization is to check only primes as factors in this range.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-126) For instance, to check whether 37 is prime, this method divides it by the primes in the range from 2 to ⁠37{\displaystyle {\sqrt {37}}}![Image 229: {\displaystyle {\sqrt {37}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2253dfc4b293b631b7fa755e6e735fc6c40de39a)⁠, which are 2, 3, and 5. Each division produces a nonzero remainder, so 37 is indeed prime.

Although this method is simple to describe, it is impractical for testing the primality of large integers, because the number of tests that it performs [grows exponentially](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exponential_growth "Exponential growth") as a function of the number of digits of these integers.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-127) However, trial division is still used, with a smaller limit than the square root on the divisor size, to quickly discover composite numbers with small factors, before using more complicated methods on the numbers that pass this filter.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-p._220-128)

### Sieves

[![Image 230: Animation of the sieve of Eratosthenes](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/Sieve_of_Eratosthenes_animation.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sieve_of_Eratosthenes_animation.gif)

The [sieve of Eratosthenes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sieve_of_Eratosthenes "Sieve of Eratosthenes") starts with all numbers unmarked (gray). It repeatedly finds the first unmarked number, marks it as prime (dark colors) and marks its square and all later multiples as composite (lighter colors). After marking the multiples of 2 (red), 3 (green), 5 (blue), and 7 (yellow), all primes up to the square root of the table size have been processed, and all remaining unmarked numbers (11, 13, etc.) are marked as primes (magenta).

Main article: [Sieve of Eratosthenes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sieve_of_Eratosthenes "Sieve of Eratosthenes")

Before computers, [mathematical tables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_table "Mathematical table") listing all of the primes or prime factorizations up to a given limit were commonly printed.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-129) The oldest known method for generating a list of primes is called the sieve of Eratosthenes.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-130) The animation shows an optimized variant of this method.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-131) Another more asymptotically efficient sieving method for the same problem is the [sieve of Atkin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sieve_of_Atkin "Sieve of Atkin").[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-132) In advanced mathematics, [sieve theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sieve_theory "Sieve theory") applies similar methods to other problems.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-133)

### Primality testing versus primality proving

Some of the fastest modern tests for whether an arbitrary given number ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 231: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ is prime are [probabilistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probabilistic_algorithm "Probabilistic algorithm") (or [Monte Carlo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monte_Carlo_algorithm "Monte Carlo algorithm")) algorithms, meaning that they have a small random chance of producing an incorrect answer.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-hromkovic-134) For instance the [Solovay–Strassen primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solovay%E2%80%93Strassen_primality_test "Solovay–Strassen primality test") on a given number ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 232: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ chooses a number ⁠a{\displaystyle a}![Image 233: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc)⁠ randomly from 2 through p−2{\displaystyle p-2}![Image 234: {\displaystyle p-2}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54de64bfce933aaf8b8317dc54f927ef25c96774) and uses [modular exponentiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_exponentiation "Modular exponentiation") to check whether a(p−1)/2±1{\displaystyle a^{(p-1)/2}\pm 1}![Image 235: {\displaystyle a^{(p-1)/2}\pm 1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/32e41c9364089b9540708ee3514f73b1bfa62a88) is divisible by ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 236: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠.[[c]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-135) If so, it answers yes and otherwise it answers no. If ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 237: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ really is prime, it will always answer yes, but if ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 238: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠ is composite then it answers yes with probability at most 1/2 and no with probability at least 1/2.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-koblitz-136) If this test is repeated ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 239: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ times on the same number, the probability that a composite number could pass the test every time is at most ⁠1/2 n{\displaystyle 1/2^{n}}![Image 240: {\displaystyle 1/2^{n}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/afcca0eda567be34036ddbb204671dc6aee9975f)⁠. Because this decreases exponentially with the number of tests, it provides high confidence (although not certainty) that a number that passes the repeated test is prime. On the other hand, if the test ever fails, then the number is certainly composite.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-137) A composite number that passes such a test is called a [pseudoprime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudoprime "Pseudoprime").[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-koblitz-136)

In contrast, some other algorithms guarantee that their answer will always be correct: primes will always be determined to be prime and composites will always be determined to be composite. For instance, this is true of trial division. The algorithms with guaranteed-correct output include both [deterministic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deterministic_algorithm "Deterministic algorithm") (non-random) algorithms, such as the [AKS primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AKS_primality_test "AKS primality test"),[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-tao-aks-138) and randomized [Las Vegas algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Las_Vegas_algorithm "Las Vegas algorithm") where the random choices made by the algorithm do not affect its final answer, such as some variations of [elliptic curve primality proving](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptic_curve_primality_proving "Elliptic curve primality proving").[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-hromkovic-134) When the elliptic curve method concludes that a number is prime, it provides [primality certificate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primality_certificate "Primality certificate") that can be verified quickly.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-atkin-morain-139) The elliptic curve primality test is the fastest in practice of the guaranteed-correct primality tests, but its runtime analysis is based on [heuristic arguments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heuristic_argument "Heuristic argument") rather than rigorous proofs. The [AKS primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AKS_primality_test "AKS primality test") has mathematically proven time complexity, but is slower than elliptic curve primality proving in practice.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-morain-140) These methods can be used to generate large random prime numbers, by generating and testing random numbers until finding one that is prime; when doing this, a faster probabilistic test can quickly eliminate most composite numbers before a guaranteed-correct algorithm is used to verify that the remaining numbers are prime.[[d]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-141)

The following table lists some of these tests. Their running time is given in terms of ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 241: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠, the number to be tested and, for probabilistic algorithms, the number ⁠k{\displaystyle k}![Image 242: {\displaystyle k}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40)⁠ of tests performed. Moreover, ε{\displaystyle \varepsilon }![Image 243: {\displaystyle \varepsilon }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a30c89172e5b88edbd45d3e2772c7f5e562e5173) is an arbitrarily small positive number, and log is the [logarithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logarithm "Logarithm") to an unspecified base. The [big O notation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_O_notation "Big O notation") means that each time bound should be multiplied by a [constant factor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constant_factor "Constant factor") to convert it from dimensionless units to units of time; this factor depends on implementation details such as the type of computer used to run the algorithm, but not on the input parameters ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 244: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ and ⁠k{\displaystyle k}![Image 245: {\displaystyle k}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40)⁠.

| Test | Developed in | Type | Running time | Notes | References |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| [AKS primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AKS_primality_test "AKS primality test") | 2002 | deterministic | O((log⁡n)6+ε){\displaystyle O((\log n)^{6+\varepsilon })}![Image 246: {\displaystyle O((\log n)^{6+\varepsilon })}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3fc16469e3af6c2a0827c49a1bc042109f80f893) |  | [[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-tao-aks-138)[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-142) |
| [Elliptic curve primality proving](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptic_curve_primality_proving "Elliptic curve primality proving") | 1986 | Las Vegas | O((log⁡n)4+ε){\displaystyle O((\log n)^{4+\varepsilon })}![Image 247: {\displaystyle O((\log n)^{4+\varepsilon })}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1dfc882e3d4fafd81c7e758315ce91a8100bd432)_heuristically_ |  | [[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-morain-140) |
| [Baillie–PSW primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baillie%E2%80%93PSW_primality_test "Baillie–PSW primality test") | 1980 | Monte Carlo | O((log⁡n)2+ε){\displaystyle O((\log n)^{2+\varepsilon })}![Image 248: {\displaystyle O((\log n)^{2+\varepsilon })}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/26335494299bcaa20a6732274e174f5eaa8461e3) |  | [[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-PSW-143)[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-lpsp-144) |

| [Solovay–Strassen primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solovay%E2%80%93Strassen_primality_test "Solovay–Strassen primality test") | 1977 | Monte Carlo | O(k(log⁡n)2+ε){\displaystyle O(k(\log n)^{2+\varepsilon })}![Image 251: {\displaystyle O(k(\log n)^{2+\varepsilon })}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c0341577e6a6f01b2c97fb60b0585bcad6ecbdc8) | error probability 2−k{\displaystyle 2^{-k}}![Image 252: {\displaystyle 2^{-k}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d5ba5275e798107d7738afeb76e2bdb91cd37e0f) | [[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-monier-145) |

### Special-purpose algorithms and the largest known prime

Further information: [List of prime numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_prime_numbers "List of prime numbers")

In addition to the aforementioned tests that apply to any natural number, some numbers of a special form can be tested for primality more quickly. For example, the [Lucas–Lehmer primality test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucas%E2%80%93Lehmer_primality_test "Lucas–Lehmer primality test") can determine whether a [Mersenne number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mersenne_number "Mersenne number") (one less than a [power of two](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_of_two "Power of two")) is prime, deterministically, in the same time as a single iteration of the Miller–Rabin test.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-146) This is why since 1992 (as of October 2024[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Prime_number&action=edit)) the [largest _known_ prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Largest_known_prime "Largest known prime") has always been a Mersenne prime.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-147) It is conjectured that there are infinitely many Mersenne primes.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-148)

The following table gives the largest known primes of various types. Some of these primes have been found using [distributed computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Distributed_computing "Distributed computing"). In 2009, the [Great Internet Mersenne Prime Search](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Internet_Mersenne_Prime_Search "Great Internet Mersenne Prime Search") project was awarded a US$100,000 prize for first discovering a prime with at least 10 million digits.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-149) The [Electronic Frontier Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_Frontier_Foundation "Electronic Frontier Foundation") also offers $150,000 and $250,000 for primes with at least 100 million digits and 1 billion digits, respectively.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-150)

| Type | Prime | Number of decimal digits | Date | Found by |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| [Mersenne prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mersenne_prime "Mersenne prime") | 2 136,279,841 − 1 | 41,024,320 | October 12, 2024[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-GIMPS-2024-1) | Luke Durant, [Great Internet Mersenne Prime Search](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Internet_Mersenne_Prime_Search "Great Internet Mersenne Prime Search") |
| [Proth prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proth_prime "Proth prime") | 10,223 × 2 31,172,165 + 1 | 9,383,761 | October 31, 2016[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-151) | Péter Szabolcs, [PrimeGrid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PrimeGrid "PrimeGrid")[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-152) |
| [factorial prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Factorial_prime "Factorial prime") | 208,003! − 1 | 1,015,843 | July 2016 | Sou Fukui[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-153) |
| [primorial prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primorial_prime "Primorial prime")[[e]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-155) | 1,098,133# − 1 | 476,311 | March 2012 | James P. Burt, [PrimeGrid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PrimeGrid "PrimeGrid")[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-156) |
| [twin primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twin_prime "Twin prime") | 2,996,863,034,895 × 2 1,290,000 ± 1 | 388,342 | September 2016 | Tom Greer, [PrimeGrid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PrimeGrid "PrimeGrid")[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-157) |

### Integer factorization

Main article: [Integer factorization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer_factorization "Integer factorization")

Given a composite integer ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 253: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠, the task of providing one (or all) prime factors is referred to as _factorization_ of ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 254: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠. It is significantly more difficult than primality testing,[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-158) and although many factorization algorithms are known, they are slower than the fastest primality testing methods. Trial division and [Pollard's rho algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pollard%27s_rho_algorithm "Pollard's rho algorithm") can be used to find very small factors of ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 255: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠,[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-p._220-128) and [elliptic curve factorization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptic_curve_factorization "Elliptic curve factorization") can be effective when ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 256: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ has factors of moderate size.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-159) Methods suitable for arbitrary large numbers that do not depend on the size of its factors include the [quadratic sieve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_sieve "Quadratic sieve") and [general number field sieve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_number_field_sieve "General number field sieve"). As with primality testing, there are also factorization algorithms that require their input to have a special form, including the [special number field sieve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_number_field_sieve "Special number field sieve").[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-160) As of December 2019[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Prime_number&action=edit) the [largest number known to have been factored](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer_factorization_records "Integer factorization records") by a general-purpose algorithm is [RSA-240](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RSA-240 "RSA-240"), which has 240 decimal digits (795 bits) and is the product of two large primes.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-161)

[Shor's algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shor%27s_algorithm "Shor's algorithm") can factor any integer in a polynomial number of steps on a [quantum computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_computer "Quantum computer").[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-162) However, current technology can only run this algorithm for very small numbers. As of October 2012[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Prime_number&action=edit), the largest number that has been factored by a quantum computer running Shor's algorithm is 21.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-163)

### Other computational applications

Several [public-key cryptography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public-key_cryptography "Public-key cryptography") algorithms, such as [RSA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RSA_(algorithm) "RSA (algorithm)") and the [Diffie–Hellman key exchange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diffie%E2%80%93Hellman_key_exchange "Diffie–Hellman key exchange"), are based on large prime numbers (2048-[bit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bit "Bit") primes are common).[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-164) RSA relies on the assumption that it is much easier (that is, more efficient) to perform the multiplication of two (large) numbers ⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 257: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠ and ⁠y{\displaystyle y}![Image 258: {\displaystyle y}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b8a6208ec717213d4317e666f1ae872e00620a0d)⁠ than to calculate ⁠x{\displaystyle x}![Image 259: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)⁠ and ⁠y{\displaystyle y}![Image 260: {\displaystyle y}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b8a6208ec717213d4317e666f1ae872e00620a0d)⁠ (assumed [coprime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coprime "Coprime")) if only the product x y{\displaystyle xy}![Image 261: {\displaystyle xy}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c72eb345e496513fb8b2fa4aa8c4d89b855f9a01) is known.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-ent-7-35) The Diffie–Hellman key exchange relies on the fact that there are efficient algorithms for [modular exponentiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_exponentiation "Modular exponentiation") (computing ⁠a b mod c{\displaystyle a^{b}{\bmod {c}}}![Image 262: {\displaystyle a^{b}{\bmod {c}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7cdcfa653d33b1ef6fa21a5a5fc365f6a7e0dea0)⁠), while the reverse operation (the [discrete logarithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Discrete_logarithm "Discrete logarithm")) is thought to be a hard problem.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-165)

Prime numbers are frequently used for [hash tables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hash_table "Hash table"). For instance the original method of Carter and Wegman for [universal hashing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universal_hashing "Universal hashing") was based on computing [hash functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hash_function "Hash function") by choosing random [linear functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_function "Linear function") modulo large prime numbers. Carter and Wegman generalized this method to [⁠k{\displaystyle k}![Image 263: {\displaystyle k}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40)⁠-independent hashing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K-independent_hashing "K-independent hashing") by using higher-degree polynomials, again modulo large primes.[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-166) As well as in the hash function, prime numbers are used for the hash table size in [quadratic probing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_probing "Quadratic probing") based hash tables to ensure that the probe sequence covers the whole table.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-167)

Some [checksum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Checksum "Checksum") methods are based on the mathematics of prime numbers. For instance the checksums used in [International Standard Book Numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number "International Standard Book Number") are defined by taking the rest of the number modulo 11, a prime number. Because 11 is prime this method can detect both single-digit errors and transpositions of adjacent digits.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-168) Another checksum method, [Adler-32](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adler-32 "Adler-32"), uses arithmetic modulo 65521, the largest prime number less than ⁠2 16{\displaystyle 2^{16}}![Image 264: {\displaystyle 2^{16}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e8e0dd3c0e42794174d2dbcb9a3ee2c6d69299d4)⁠.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-169) Prime numbers are also used in [pseudorandom number generators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudorandom_number_generator "Pseudorandom number generator") including [linear congruential generators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_congruential_generator "Linear congruential generator")[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-170) and the [Mersenne Twister](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mersenne_Twister "Mersenne Twister").[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-171)

Other applications
------------------

Prime numbers are of central importance to number theory but also have many applications to other areas within mathematics, including [abstract algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstract_algebra "Abstract algebra") and elementary geometry. For example, it is possible to place prime numbers of points in a two-dimensional grid so that [no three are in a line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/No-three-in-line_problem "No-three-in-line problem"), or so that every triangle formed by three of the points [has large area](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heilbronn_triangle_problem "Heilbronn triangle problem").[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-172) Another example is [Eisenstein's criterion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eisenstein%27s_criterion "Eisenstein's criterion"), a test for whether a [polynomial is irreducible](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irreducible_polynomial "Irreducible polynomial") based on divisibility of its coefficients by a prime number and its square.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-173)

[![Image 265](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Sum_of_knots3.svg/250px-Sum_of_knots3.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sum_of_knots3.svg)

The connected sum of two prime knots

The concept of a prime number is so important that it has been generalized in different ways in various branches of mathematics. Generally, "prime" indicates minimality or indecomposability, in an appropriate sense. For example, the [prime field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_field "Prime field") of a given field is its smallest subfield that contains both 0 and 1. It is either the field of rational numbers or a [finite field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finite_field "Finite field") with a prime number of elements, whence the name.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-174) Often a second, additional meaning is intended by using the word prime, namely that any object can be, essentially uniquely, decomposed into its prime components. For example, in [knot theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knot_theory "Knot theory"), a [prime knot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_knot "Prime knot") is a [knot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knot_(mathematics) "Knot (mathematics)") that is indecomposable in the sense that it cannot be written as the [connected sum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Connected_sum "Connected sum") of two nontrivial knots. Any knot can be uniquely expressed as a connected sum of prime knots.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-175) The [prime decomposition of 3-manifolds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_decomposition_(3-manifold) "Prime decomposition (3-manifold)") is another example of this type.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-176)

Beyond mathematics and computing, prime numbers have potential connections to [quantum mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_mechanics "Quantum mechanics"), and have been used metaphorically in the arts and literature. They have also been used in [evolutionary biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_biology "Evolutionary biology") to explain the life cycles of [cicadas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cicada "Cicada").

### Constructible polygons and polygon partitions

[![Image 266: Construction of a regular pentagon using straightedge and compass](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Pentagon_construct.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pentagon_construct.gif)

Construction of a regular pentagon using straightedge and compass. This is only possible because 5 is a [Fermat prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat_prime "Fermat prime").

[Fermat primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat_prime "Fermat prime") are primes of the form

F k=2 2 k+1,{\displaystyle F_{k}=2^{2^{k}}+1,}![Image 267: {\displaystyle F_{k}=2^{2^{k}}+1,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3cc6edcaa62bbd72af19a441f8cdba0629f37eb0)
with ⁠k{\displaystyle k}![Image 268: {\displaystyle k}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40)⁠ a [nonnegative integer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nonnegative_integer "Nonnegative integer").[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-177) They are named after [Pierre de Fermat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat "Pierre de Fermat"), who conjectured that all such numbers are prime. The first five of these numbers – 3, 5, 17, 257, and 65,537 – are prime,[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-kls-178) but F 5{\displaystyle F_{5}}![Image 269: {\displaystyle F_{5}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c64647603e8358bf2b07099963d5ac2d8b75ee9a) is composite and so are all other Fermat numbers that have been verified as of 2017.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-179) A [regular ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 270: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠-gon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_polygon "Regular polygon") is [constructible using straightedge and compass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constructible_polygon "Constructible polygon") if and only if the odd prime factors of ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 271: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ (if any) are distinct Fermat primes.[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-kls-178) Likewise, a regular ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 272: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠-gon may be constructed using straightedge, compass, and an [angle trisector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angle_trisection "Angle trisection") if and only if the prime factors of [⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 273: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_polygon "Regular polygon") are any number of copies of 2 or 3 together with a (possibly empty) set of distinct [Pierpont primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierpont_prime "Pierpont prime"), primes of the form ⁠2 a 3 b+1{\displaystyle 2^{a}3^{b}+1}![Image 274: {\displaystyle 2^{a}3^{b}+1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d06f1cc50f358fed2f88c9789d2a15fc5c003dc5)⁠.[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-180)

It is possible to partition any convex polygon into ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 275: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ smaller convex polygons of equal area and equal perimeter, when ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 276: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠ is a [power of a prime number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_power "Prime power"), but this is not known for other values of ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 277: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-181)

### Quantum mechanics

Beginning with the work of [Hugh Montgomery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hugh_Lowell_Montgomery "Hugh Lowell Montgomery") and [Freeman Dyson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freeman_Dyson "Freeman Dyson") in the 1970s, mathematicians and physicists have speculated that the zeros of the Riemann zeta function are connected to the energy levels of [quantum systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_system "Quantum system").[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-182)[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-183) Prime numbers are also significant in [quantum information science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_information_science "Quantum information science"), thanks to mathematical structures such as [mutually unbiased bases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutually_unbiased_bases "Mutually unbiased bases") and [symmetric informationally complete positive-operator-valued measures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SIC-POVM "SIC-POVM").[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-184)[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-185)

### Biology

The evolutionary strategy used by [cicadas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cicada "Cicada") of the genus _[Magicicada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magicicada "Magicicada")_ makes use of prime numbers.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-186) These insects spend most of their lives as [grubs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Larva "Larva") underground. They only pupate and then emerge from their burrows after 7, 13 or 17 years, at which point they fly about, breed, and then die after a few weeks at most. Biologists theorize that these prime-numbered breeding cycle lengths have evolved in order to prevent predators from synchronizing with these cycles.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-187)[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-188) In contrast, the multi-year periods between flowering in [bamboo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bamboo "Bamboo") plants are hypothesized to be [smooth numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smooth_number "Smooth number"), having only small prime numbers in their factorizations.[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-189)

### Arts and literature

Prime numbers have influenced many artists and writers. The French [composer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Composer "Composer")[Olivier Messiaen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olivier_Messiaen "Olivier Messiaen") used prime numbers to create ametrical music through "natural phenomena". In works such as _[La Nativité du Seigneur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/La\_Nativit%C3%A9\_du\_Seigneur "La Nativité du Seigneur")_ (1935) and _[Quatre études de rythme](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quatre\_%C3%A9tudes\_de\_rythme "Quatre études de rythme")_ (1949–1950), he simultaneously employs motifs with lengths given by different prime numbers to create unpredictable rhythms: the primes 41, 43, 47 and 53 appear in the third étude, "Neumes rythmiques". According to Messiaen this way of composing was "inspired by the movements of nature, movements of free and unequal durations".[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-190)

In his science fiction novel _[Contact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contact\_(novel) "Contact (novel)")_, scientist [Carl Sagan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Sagan "Carl Sagan") suggested that prime factorization could be used as a means of establishing two-dimensional image planes in communications with aliens, an idea that he had first developed informally with American astronomer [Frank Drake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_Drake "Frank Drake") in 1975.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-191) In the novel _[The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Night-Time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Curious\_Incident\_of\_the\_Dog\_in\_the\_Night-Time "The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Night-Time")_ by [Mark Haddon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Haddon "Mark Haddon"), the narrator arranges the sections of the story by consecutive prime numbers as a way to convey the mental state of its main character, a mathematically gifted teen with [Asperger syndrome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asperger_syndrome "Asperger syndrome").[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-192) Prime numbers are used as a metaphor for loneliness and isolation in the [Paolo Giordano](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paolo_Giordano "Paolo Giordano") novel _[The Solitude of Prime Numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Solitude\_of\_Prime\_Numbers\_(novel) "The Solitude of Prime Numbers (novel)")_, in which they are portrayed as "outsiders" among integers.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-193)

2.   ^ [_**a**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_ref-pure_34-0)[_**b**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_ref-pure_34-1)For instance, Beiler writes that number theorist [Ernst Kummer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Kummer "Ernst Kummer") loved his [ideal numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_number "Ideal number"), closely related to the primes, "because they had not soiled themselves with any practical applications",[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-32) and Katz writes that [Edmund Landau](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edmund_Landau "Edmund Landau"), known for his work on the distribution of primes, "loathed practical applications of mathematics", and for this reason avoided subjects such as [geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry "Geometry") that had already shown themselves to be useful.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-33)
3.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_ref-135)**In this test, the ±1{\displaystyle \pm 1}![Image 278: {\displaystyle \pm 1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0bfeaa85da53ad1947d8000926cfea33827ef1e0) term is negative if ⁠a{\displaystyle a}![Image 279: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc)⁠ is a square modulo the given (supposed) prime ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 280: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠, and positive otherwise. More generally, for non-prime values of ⁠p{\displaystyle p}![Image 281: {\displaystyle p}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36)⁠, the ±1{\displaystyle \pm 1}![Image 282: {\displaystyle \pm 1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0bfeaa85da53ad1947d8000926cfea33827ef1e0) term is the (negated) [Jacobi symbol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobi_symbol "Jacobi symbol"), which can be calculated using [quadratic reciprocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_reciprocity "Quadratic reciprocity").
4.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_ref-141)**Indeed, much of the analysis of elliptic curve primality proving is based on the assumption that the input to the algorithm has already passed a probabilistic test.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-atkin-morain-139)
5.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_ref-155)**The [primorial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primorial "Primorial") function of ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 283: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠, denoted by ⁠n#{\displaystyle n\#}![Image 284: {\displaystyle n\#}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/167c3481839df4ace6689a25e170c5e0c0d5551e)⁠, yields the product of the prime numbers up to ⁠n{\displaystyle n}![Image 285: {\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b)⁠, and a [primorial prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primorial_prime "Primorial prime") is a prime of one of the forms ⁠n#±1{\displaystyle n\#\pm 1}![Image 286: {\displaystyle n\#\pm 1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5c0d1d93846f485187cb017391c93d5cc2147d0a)⁠.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number#cite_note-154)

[EMS Press](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Mathematical_Society "European Mathematical Society"). 2001 [1994].

*   [Prime Numbers](https://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p003hyf5) on [_In Our Time_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/In_Our_Time_(radio_series) "In Our Time (radio series)") at the [BBC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBC "BBC").
*   "[Teacher package: Prime numbers](http://plus.maths.org/issue49/package/index.html)" from _Plus_, December 1, 2008, produced by the Millennium Mathematics Project at the University of Cambridge.

### Generators and calculators

*   [Prime factors calculator](http://www.javascripter.net/math/calculators/primefactorscalculator.htm) can factorize any positive integer up to 20 digits.
*   [Fast Online primality test with factorization](https://www.alpertron.com.ar/ECM.HTM) makes use of the Elliptic Curve Method (up to thousand-digits numbers, requires Java).
*   [Huge database of prime numbers](http://www.bigprimes.net/).
*   [Prime Numbers up to 1 trillion](http://www.primos.mat.br/indexen.html). [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20210227001026/http://www.primos.mat.br/indexen.html) 2021-02-27 at the [Wayback Machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wayback_Machine "Wayback Machine").
